    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14983329072780506263">Considered by many to be her masterpiece, Edith Wharton's epic work is a scathing yet personal examination of the exploits and follies of the modern upper class. As she unfolds the story of Undine Spragg, from New York to Europe, Wharton affords us a detailed glimpse of what might be called the interior décor of this America and its nouveau riche fringes. Through a heroine</span>
  <span id="freeText14983329072780506263" style="display:none">Considered by many to be her masterpiece, Edith Wharton's epic work is a scathing yet personal examination of the exploits and follies of the modern upper class. As she unfolds the story of Undine Spragg, from New York to Europe, Wharton affords us a detailed glimpse of what might be called the interior décor of this America and its nouveau riche fringes. Through a heroine who is as vain, spoiled, and selfish as she is irresistibly fascinating, and through a most intricate and satisfying plot that follows Undine's marriages and affairs, she conveys a vision of social behavior that is both supremely informed and supremely disenchanted. <i>- Anita Brookner</i></span>

The plot moves along at a good clip, and if you don't read any introductory essays with spoilers, there's a fair amoun<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I wouldn't say it's dry at all. The plot moves along at a good clip, and if you don't read any introductory essays with spoilers, there's a fair amount of suspense, with a few twists toward the end. I think the character of Undine Spragg will inspire some lively conversation!<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<span class='communityAnswererName'>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Undine is an elemental water spirit, a mythological creature. Truly a perfect name for our unsympathetic heroine, with her glorious red hair, pale ski<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Undine is an elemental water spirit, a mythological creature. Truly a perfect name for our unsympathetic heroine, with her glorious red hair, pale skin, and green eyes. Except that Wharton has Undine's parents naming her after a patented hair curling device briefly peddled by her father. Her son Paul is cared for by Ralph's mother and sister before Undine sends for him.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1097642486" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1097642486" />
    <a title="Petra-X" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x"><img alt="Petra-X" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603179398p2/1237196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1097642486?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petra-X" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petra-X" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x">Petra-X</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=2014-read">2014-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=2014-reviews">2014-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1097642486" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3316086606390699999">It was fashionable at one time to send rich American girls who had everything over to Europe in order to acquire a title from an impoverished aristocrat who was none too fussy about his bride so long as she came with a very generous papa. Some of them, like the extremely despicable Lady Rose Astor <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[How else can one describe a racist, anti-semitic bff of Henry Ford and other Nazi sympathisers? <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> really became part of their adoptive country and others, like our heroine,</span>
  <span id="freeText3316086606390699999" style="display:none">It was fashionable at one time to send rich American girls who had everything over to Europe in order to acquire a title from an impoverished aristocrat who was none too fussy about his bride so long as she came with a very generous papa. Some of them, like the extremely despicable Lady Rose Astor <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[How else can one describe a racist, anti-semitic bff of Henry Ford and other Nazi sympathisers? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> really became part of their adoptive country and others, like our heroine, Undine Spragg didn't.<br /><br />Undine wasn't top-drawer wealthy to begin with, only middling, and she had a past, but that didn't stop her pursuit of wealth, haute couture, a sparkling social life and compliments. She came from Apex and you might think reached her apex on marrying a French Marquis and gaining a title and a historic palace, but no, he wasn't rich enough for her. And so displaying all the worst characteristics of an American (as described by the Marquis - shallow, no feeling for history or art, everything is valued according to its monetary worth and has to be new) she tries to sell, or persuade the Marquis to sell, tapestries given by Louis IVX to his family. In doing so she meets her first husband again, now a billionaire, and so a quickie divorce at midnight is arranged with a wedding minutes later, and she is happy as happy could be. All that money!<br /><br />Her husband, provider of this unlimited largesse, lives if not to regret it, at least to make the best of it, which is more than can be said for her second husband, father of her only child. He shot himself. The husband pals up to this child, whom Undine scarcely knows and cares even less about. The husband knows that if he had no money, he too would not bask in her affections but be subject to the same cold, brittle light Undine regards her son with and determines to do his best for the boy. <br /><br />It's quite a brilliant book, related closely to the French genre of beautiful women and the machinations they get up to in pursuit of wealth. But the French are moralistic and the woman always gets her comeuppance, whether it be the pox, age, or the mirror on the wall keeps telling her there are younger beauties that men will pay top dollar for, not old whores past their sell-by date. Edith Wharton being an American does not feel the need for this sort of ending. Her heroine, nasty as she is has achieved the American dream of social-climbing and wealth - anyone can get to the top. And there she leaves her narcissistic billionairess. <br /><br />Well-written, and very enjoyable. The only flaw for me was the headlong drive for money and social domination was not tempered by any softness or virtues in the heroine. She lacked humanity. You couldn't identify with her as you could with, say, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2175.Madame_Bovary" title="Madame Bovary by Gustave Flaubert" rel="nofollow noopener">Madame Bovary</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3316086606390699999" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1950965211" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1950965211" />
    <a title="Vessey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30910845-vessey"><img alt="Vessey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1511166278p2/30910845.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1950965211?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vessey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vessey" href="/user/show/30910845-vessey">Vessey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Vessey by: </span>
      Candi
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30910845-vessey?shelf=dark">dark</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30910845-vessey?shelf=4-stars">4-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1950965211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15462896731973782391">SPOILERS<br/><br/><b>Social gold does not always glitter</b><br/><br/>Edith Wharton did not have a happy life. Nor do her characters. What is happiness anyway, if not merely a part of our lives, something we all pursue, but rarely, if ever, possess in a clean, full form? We are destined to fail. We are imperfect by design. And Undine Spragg is one of the most imperfect characters I have come across. Actually, <i>imperfect</i> is an understatement. She is a walking disaster. A woman almost completely devoid of empathy and self-re</span>
  <span id="freeText15462896731973782391" style="display:none">SPOILERS<br /><br /><b>Social gold does not always glitter</b><br /><br />Edith Wharton did not have a happy life. Nor do her characters. What is happiness anyway, if not merely a part of our lives, something we all pursue, but rarely, if ever, possess in a clean, full form? We are destined to fail. We are imperfect by design. And Undine Spragg is one of the most imperfect characters I have come across. Actually, <i>imperfect</i> is an understatement. She is a walking disaster. A woman almost completely devoid of empathy and self-respect. She is forever entrapped in her search for greatness, but nothing she achieves is ever enough. As soon as I was finished, I told my friend Jeffrey: “What is the point of achieving what you dream of if you can never enjoy it, because you are always so consumed with what you don’t have, if the very reason for wanting what you do is that you don’t have it and once you have it, you find yourself bored and angry and it turns out that nothing has really changed? I think that the reason why the protagonist – I should say the antagonist, really - can never be happy with her achievements is her lack of desire to share them. And, of course, they lose their meaning. She merely wants to be admired and indulged and not bothered with anything, to not think and be responsible for anyone and anything. She has no sense of self. She wants to be treated like an object, like all she exists for is to be admired from afar, like she has no substance, in the same way a doll or a sculpture is admired, like she is empty. And this is why her life is empty too.” As my friend Sidharth says, life needs life to see itself. <br /><br /><i>
  <b>It was admiration, not love, that she wanted. She wanted to enjoy herself, and her conception of enjoyment was publicity, promiscuity – the band, the banners, the crowd, the close contact of covetous impulses, and the sense of walking among them in cool security..A stranger – that was what she had always been to him. So malleable outwardly, she had remained insensible to the touch of the heart.</b>
</i><br /><br />In her mind there is no place for considerations, scruples, limitations. <br /><br /><i>
  <b>It was impossible for Undine to understand a social organization which did not regard the indulging of woman as its first purpose</b>
</i><br /><br />She completely lacks depth. She combines passionate desires with passionless nature. A combination that leads to the downfall of everyone who loves her and her own personal hell. But is it merely her fault? <br /><br /><i>
  <b>The real paradox is the fact that the men who make, materially, the biggest sacrifices for their women, should do least for them ideally and romantically. And what’s the result – how do the women avenge themselves? All my sympathy’s with them, poor deluded dears, when I see their fallacious little attempts to trick out the leavings tossed them by the preoccupied male – the money and the motors and the clothes – and pretend to themselves and each other that that’s what really constitutes life! Oh, I know what you’re going to say – it’s less and less of a pretence with them, I grant you; they’re more and more succumbing to the force of the suggestion; but here and there I fancy there’s one who still sees through the humbug, and knows that money and motors and clothes are simply the big bribe she’s paid for keeping out of some man’s way!’<br /><br />‘And is Undine one of the exceptions?’<br /><br />Her companion took the shot with a smile. ‘No – she’s a monstrously perfect result of the system: the completest proof of its triumph. It’s Ralph who’s the victim and the exception.</b>
</i><br /><br />Unlike others, Undine doesn’t learn any lessons, doesn’t go through any profound changes. She keeps being a soulless, mindless force of nature, sucking the life out of everyone close to her. This book, just like <i>The Age of Innocence</i>, reveals the truth that often stays unsaid. That when women are belittled, men are no less harmed. Because each right entails with itself a responsibility and vice versa. When there is no equality, when there is no partnership, there are no winners. Only destruction. And by refusing to admit it, we become our worst enemies. Undine is a woman of her time. But is Ralph truly an exception? There is no doubt about how much he loves her and that he wants to give her much more than just material goods, that he wants her to be an essential part of his life. However, he misses to realize that indulging someone isn’t enough to create a real partnership. By tolerating and accepting everything she does – to him, to their child – he is no less of a supporter of the vicious circle. He completely loses his spirit. <br /><br /><i>
  <b>The flame of love that had played about his passion for his wife had died down to its embers; all the transfiguring hopes and illusions were gone, but they had left an unquenchable ache for her nearness, her smile, her touch. His life had come to be nothing but a long effort to win these mercies by one concession after another: the sacrifice of his literary projects, the exchange of his profession for an uncongenial business, and the incessant struggle to make enough money to satisfy her increasing exactions. That was where the ‘call’ had led him …</b>
</i><br /><br />He spends his whole life diminishing himself and exalting her. This adoring, gentle, maybe too good to be true husband feeds the abyss as much as every self-centered lover of boot-licking out there. Not having appreciation for yourself is as bad as not having it for the person next to you. The lack of sense of self is probably the only thing he truly shares with Undine. Even in the end, when he finally has a reaction, he takes down not her, but himself. He destroys himself and the future of his son.<br /><br />The distinction between winner and loser, abuser and victim, is not always quite clear. To me Ralph and Undine, albeit very different, are two sides of the same coin. Products of an ill system that either corrupts or crashes you. Unlike the ending of <i>The Age of Innocence</i>, the ending of <i>The Custom of the Country</i> doesn’t leave us with a glimmer of hope. It is perfect and eloquent in its bleakness. Edith Wharton strips the body of the late XIX century New York society of its brilliant clothing, separates the skin from the bones and turns the bones into dust. There is no happiness in this story, but there is truth. And, as imperfect as we are, it is up to us whether we shall let it be <i>our</i> truth or find that glimmer of hope, after all.<br /><br />My thanks to Candi for the recommendation! :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15462896731973782391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_8739940" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8739940" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/419287-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289534648p2/419287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8739940?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/419287-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">mommy's little monsters; unsympathetic heroines; american girls</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jessica by: </span>
      sister rachel; mother dear
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=crazy-ladies">crazy-ladies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=groups-of-people">groups-of-people</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=here-is-new-york">here-is-new-york</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=leetle-boys">leetle-boys</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=chicklits">chicklits</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8739940" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11286371686350576730">So I had totally committed my schedule to having lengthy tea with a brilliant Oxford professor of incredible intelligence, unsurpassed insight, and fabled dry wit. And while I know that my extended afternoon with Dr. George Eliot would have proven to be a fascinating and immensely edifying experience that I would've remembered for the rest of my life, I still did the bad thing and just blew her off. Yes, I ditched the eminent Dr. Eliot to drink ice cream sodas and read celebrity gossip magazines</span>
  <span id="freeText11286371686350576730" style="display:none">So I had totally committed my schedule to having lengthy tea with a brilliant Oxford professor of incredible intelligence, unsurpassed insight, and fabled dry wit. And while I know that my extended afternoon with Dr. George Eliot would have proven to be a fascinating and immensely edifying experience that I would've remembered for the rest of my life, I still did the bad thing and just blew her off. Yes, I ditched the eminent Dr. Eliot to drink ice cream sodas and read celebrity gossip magazines with a bubbly, divorced Gibson Girl who's gorgeous but dreadful, who's got all the new fashions and knows the most ruthless jokes. And who can really blame me for breaking my engagement? I'm an American! I can't help it! We just wanna have fun. We want things to be sexy and dazzling, and brand spanking NEW. And okay, this book's not <i>new</i> exactly (1913), but it sure reads that way. I can't improve on my mother's description of <i>The Custom of the Country</i> as "Henry James meets Candace Bushnell," except to recommend it also to fans of <i>Gossip Girl</i> and similar treats.<br /><br />Edith Wharton is truly a fabulous writer, and her style's as gorgeous as the gilded world of the rich she describes. The special thing about this particular book of hers is its repellent anti-heroine, the wonderfully named and well-initialed society beauty Undine Spragg. Undine Spragg makes Emma Bovary look like a sweet, sharp young lady who'd be good BFF material. Wharton's unsubtle point that society (Society?) creates monsters like Undine is not so heavy-handed that the pace or plot gets slowed down. While there are definitely some tragic, Whartonian events in here, the whole novel for me felt both like tragedy and comedy, and her unsympathetic heroine bit works so successfully that the novel profits hugely from that ambivalence. The book ends with a marriage, and is tragic in this weird and smart sense that the hero doesn't realize there has been any tragedy. It's a good good book, truly. I had a swell time.<br /><br />Can I just say now that I LOVE Edith Wharton? I LOVE Edith Wharton. I just think she's great. She sure could write, man. <i>The Custom of the Country</i> is very specifically about a historical moment and certain issues -- American identity, wealth, the Woman Question, etc. -- but it's in the Great Lit section because the things she's getting into still matter a lot. Undine's one of the most beautiful characters in literature, and it's no accident that she's got one of the most memorably lousy personalities. Wharton makes her very human, though, and weirdly sympathetic, while tying the tragedy that is Undine to ideas about the social role of women and the effects of that role on individuals. So <i>The Custom of the Country</i> is, in that way, a successful social novel, because Wharton gets her political concerns across through -- not in spite of -- the story's unfolding.<br /><br />But <i>The Custom of the Country</i> is, more than a great social novel, a great Society novel. "Society" being what Americans tried to put together before we got Hollywood, this is a sort of a proto-<i>Valley of the Dolls</i> celebrity cautionary tale, which is another gigantic point in its favor. I also got all riled up when Undine made it to Paris and started trying to get in with the Faubourg Saint Germain set, in the hopes that she'd bump into the main character from <i>In Search of Lost Time</i>. That unfortunately didn't happen, but it is fun to think about, in a really dumb, nerdy, middle-school English class assignment kind of way.... Edith Wharton's no Marcel Proust, but that's not necessarily a bad thing. She's no George Eliot either, so while Wharton may be considered somewhat less respectable in certain social circles, when I go out calling it's her drawing room I rush to first. It's the best decorated and most fun, and Dr. Eliot can wait, possibly forever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11286371686350576730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2065268014" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2065268014" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2065268014?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=parenting">parenting</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2065268014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4889477625010585667">Edith Wharton has fixed Henry James, whose essential problem is that he's a pain in the ass. He's smart and all, if that's what you're into, but he's never been known to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/231237802' title='My review of The Turn of the Screw' rel='nofollow noopener'>end a sentence</a> and he has this perverse <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1314594915' title='My review of Portrait of a Lady' rel='nofollow noopener'>refusal to write the interesting parts</a> of stories. It's weird, right? It's like if the Death Star blew up off screen and the movie ended with people discussing it. "That was crazy how that just blew right up, huh?" "Yeah, at first I thought we weren't going to win, but in the end we did!" </span>
  <span id="freeText4889477625010585667" style="display:none">Edith Wharton has fixed Henry James, whose essential problem is that he's a pain in the ass. He's smart and all, if that's what you're into, but he's never been known to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/231237802" title="My review of The Turn of the Screw" rel="nofollow noopener">end a sentence</a> and he has this perverse <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1314594915" title="My review of Portrait of a Lady" rel="nofollow noopener">refusal to write the interesting parts</a> of stories. It's weird, right? It's like if the Death Star blew up off screen and the movie ended with people discussing it. "That was crazy how that just blew right up, huh?" "Yeah, at first I thought we weren't going to win, but in the end we did!" Edith Wharton is like Henry James with the interesting parts.<br /><br /><i>Custom of the Country</i> follows Wharton's best character, especially if you like terrible characters: Undine Spragg, the Edmund Hillary of social climbing. She arrives in New York from the allegorical Midwestern small town of Apex, with money on her mind. "To have things had always seemed to her the first essential of existence." She's out to use her beauty to become one of the beautiful people. <br /><br />She's a social genius: brilliant at reading people, seeing what they really want, adapting her behavior to them. But she's desperately shallow - "a mote in the beam of pleasure" - and that's her hamartia. (That's a fancy word I just learned; it means fatal flaw.) If what they want is to talk about art or literature, she's bright enough to know it but in no way interested in doing it.<br /><br />So she goes through social circles, partners, cities. "She was always doubling and twisting on herself." She marries <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the hidden sea cave Ralph Marvell, a sensitive artist type with social stature but no money. She dumps him for Peter Van Degen, who has the money but no intention of divorcing his wife; she rebounds with the French aristocrat Raymond de Chelles, who makes her a marquise but will not fund her partygoing. And she ends up with who she started with, the crude, ambitious, successful Elmer Moffat. Her big secret all along has been that she capriciously married him back in Apex, in sortof a Britney Spears-in-Vegas move. In a bizarrely touching way, Moffat has loved her all along; he's pulled strings, sometimes cruelly, to keep her afloat and within reach. You find yourself almost happy for her with this match; they're perfect for each other, both entirely untroubled by morals or empathy. But even at the end, with all the money and social standing she's ever wanted, she's already grasping for the next rung of the social ladder. "She could never be an Ambassador's wife," thanks to her divorces, and in the very last sentence of the book, "she said to herself that it was the one part she was really made for." <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> "She had everything she wanted," Wharton tells us, "But she still felt, at times, that there were other things she might want if she knew about them."<br /><br />Much of this is very funny, and much more of it is very sad. Undine (pronounced, probably, UNdeen) is yet another in the long, long line of literature's shitty mothers. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[She forgets poor Paul's birthday, leaving him sitting in his nursery waiting to be taken to his own party. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> At one point she blackmails her husband with a threat to pretend to love her son. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[She demands $100,000 (to buy an annulment) or she'll take custody of him, despite having no interest whatsoever in him. The scheme drives Ralph Marvell to suicide. This plan was suggested by Elmer Moffat, in the most consequential example of his machinations. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> She is truly a piece of shit person, which didn't at all stop me from rooting for her. <br /><br />Much of this book is about divorce, and what it means for a woman; and indeed Edith Wharton wrote it as she went through her own, devastating divorce. It was a painful one, from a philandering and profligate husband - and not the only time Wharton would swap the genders while telling a story - and she never recovered from it; her social standing was permanently damaged. Instead of remarrying, at age 50, she focused on writing novels. <br /><br />So we should thank her lame husband, because if he were any better, she might never have fixed Henry James. Here's what James's psychological acuity and insight into human motivation looks like when it's engaged by someone who knows how to finish a sentence, and how to write the exciting parts. Edith Wharton was deeply influenced by him - this book is a direct, if crooked, response to his aching <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2072447452" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Washington Square</a> - but she's better than him. I love <a href="http://www.nybooks.com/articles/2007/04/26/the-house-of-edith/" title="NYRB article" rel="nofollow noopener">this judgment</a> from Edmund White: "Even in the best of Wharton, I’d hazard, there is always something slightly trashy...as though Henry James and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/951467852" title="My review of The Moonstone" rel="nofollow noopener">Wilkie Collins</a> were always struggling over her soul." Yes, that's as good a way to say it as any. She ends up combining the best of both of them into something that's better than either. She is a marvel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4889477625010585667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9584690864185683708">This book is amazing. No one writes like this anymore -- in fact, after I finished this, I had a hard time getting into a more contemporary novel, because the newer book felt so spare and empty compared to Wharton's thoughtful and lovely prose. Certain paragraphs of Custom of the Country made me stop and just admire her craft; she conveys so much depth of thought in so few sentences, with precision and elegance that I've never encountered elsewhere and could never even begin to replicate. It ble</span>
  <span id="freeText9584690864185683708" style="display:none">This book is amazing. No one writes like this anymore -- in fact, after I finished this, I had a hard time getting into a more contemporary novel, because the newer book felt so spare and empty compared to Wharton's thoughtful and lovely prose. Certain paragraphs of Custom of the Country made me stop and just admire her craft; she conveys so much depth of thought in so few sentences, with precision and elegance that I've never encountered elsewhere and could never even begin to replicate. It blew me away.<br /><br />Aside from that, the story is great fun, with its spunky, bratty heroine on a tear through Europe collecting suitors and clothing, Katamari Damacy-style. You might hate her, but you have to admire her power. And you have to admire Wharton's almost eerily modern ideology. </span>

<div id="review_760878312" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/760878312" />
    <a title="Duane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24389390-duane"><img alt="Duane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403316391p2/24389390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/760878312?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Duane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Duane" href="/user/show/24389390-duane">Duane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=reviewed-books">reviewed-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=rated-books">rated-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=american-classics">american-classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer760878312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3825818250873692060"> Someone once advised Edith Wharton, I think it was Henry James, to be successful in writing you should focus on subjects that you are familiar with and understand. For Wharton, that was New York, and the privileged upper crust society of which she was a part. Aside from Ethan Frome, her most beloved novels are three that captured the essence of this society and it's people, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17728.The_House_of_Mirth' title='The House of Mirth by Edith Wharton' rel='nofollow noopener'>The House of Mirth</a> (1905), <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26950.The_Custom_of_the_Country' title='The Custom of the Country by Edith Wharton' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Custom of the Country</a> (1913), and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53835.The_Age_of_Innocence' title='The Age of Innocence by Edith Wharton' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Age of Innocence</a> (1920).<br/><br/> The Custom of the Country pr</span>
  <span id="freeText3825818250873692060" style="display:none"> Someone once advised Edith Wharton, I think it was Henry James, to be successful in writing you should focus on subjects that you are familiar with and understand. For Wharton, that was New York, and the privileged upper crust society of which she was a part. Aside from Ethan Frome, her most beloved novels are three that captured the essence of this society and it's people, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17728.The_House_of_Mirth" title="The House of Mirth by Edith Wharton" rel="nofollow noopener">The House of Mirth</a> (1905), <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26950.The_Custom_of_the_Country" title="The Custom of the Country by Edith Wharton" rel="nofollow noopener">The Custom of the Country</a> (1913), and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53835.The_Age_of_Innocence" title="The Age of Innocence by Edith Wharton" rel="nofollow noopener">The Age of Innocence</a> (1920).<br /><br /> The Custom of the Country produced one of Wharton's most memorable characters, Miss Undine Spragg, who came to New York from a well to do, but not socially connected, family. She finally breaks into high society when she meets and eventually marries Ralph Marvell. He has the family connections but not the wealth she is hoping for, but she has stepped on that ladder of upward mobility and she takes advantage of every opportunity from that point to fulfill her dreams. If I counted right she is married four times and has at least one significant affair during the course of the novel. I don't think she was in love with anyone but herself. Yes, Undine was a vain and very shallow young woman.<br /><br /> Wharton's ability to create interesting characters, captivating plots, and prose that is incredibly beautiful, makes anything she writes a pleasure to read. I don't know if this is her best novel, many critics think it is, but it is right up there, not only with her other works, but with anything from that period. And that period includes the likes of Fitzgerald, Hemingway, and her good friend Henry James. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3825818250873692060" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_171456134" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/171456134" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/171456134?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer171456134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12412790929352261291"><br/>Edith Wharton's gift was her twenty twenty vision of the society she lived in, New York at the beginning of the 20th century. The moral of this complicated but satisfying tale seems to be that without the well established customs to be found in old Europe, people in the new world are adrift and have nothing better to aspire to than wealth and celebrity status. The irony is that her conclusions could apply to the Europe of today.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_17447023" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17447023" />
    <a title="Martine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/381149-martine"><img alt="Martine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1189863081p2/381149.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17447023?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martine" href="/user/show/381149-martine">Martine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText"> lovers of elegant early-twentieth-century fiction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=north-american">north-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=early-twentieth-century">early-twentieth-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=modern-fiction">modern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=psychological-drama">psychological-drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17447023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17554081159115382263">Think Edith Wharton only wrote novels about nice people who fall victim to society's uncongenial mores? Then <i>The Custom of the Country</i> may come as a bit of a surprise to you. Far from a dignified, morally superior character, the book's heroine, the beautiful but vulgar Undine Spragg, is a selfish monster who takes society (or rather, several different societies) head on, suffers a bit for her lack of subtlety but comes out filthy rich. Unless you're a gold-digger yourself, you'll find Undine har</span>
  <span id="freeText17554081159115382263" style="display:none">Think Edith Wharton only wrote novels about nice people who fall victim to society's uncongenial mores? Then <i>The Custom of the Country</i> may come as a bit of a surprise to you. Far from a dignified, morally superior character, the book's heroine, the beautiful but vulgar Undine Spragg, is a selfish monster who takes society (or rather, several different societies) head on, suffers a bit for her lack of subtlety but comes out filthy rich. Unless you're a gold-digger yourself, you'll find Undine hard to identify with, but that doesn't really matter, because for one thing, the story is far too interesting to care about a trifle like an unlikeable heroine, and for another, there are other characters to sympathise with. Such as Undine's poor, long-suffering husbands, to whom Wharton devotes a few beautiful, desperate chapters.<br /><br />Undine Spragg is a marvellous creation whose sense of entitlement and ruthlessness defy belief. She is as hypocritical as the 'friends' with whom she surrounds herself, disapproving of them for doing the same things she herself does without seeming to be aware of the irony. As a reader, you keep waiting for Undine to learn a lesson, but other than a dim awareness that others seem to find her rather dull once they have got over her physical beauty, she never learns a thing, let alone the harsh lesson she so deserves. She just goes on and on pressing for the things she wants, and for some reason she gets them. Most of them, anyway.<br /><br />As always, Wharton's prose is beautiful, her satire thick and marvellous. With great authority she lashes out at the different circles in which Undine moves, all of which Wharton knew from experience: Midwestern wannabes, representatives of venerable Old New York (dignified but ineffective), the Gilded Age nouveaux riches (vulgar, very vulgar, but not entirely without redeeming qualities) and the French aristocracy (dignified but hypocritical). What with Undine being so successful, <i>The Custom of the Country</i> lacks some of the tragic quality that makes <i>The House of Mirth</i> and <i>The Age of Innocence</i> so memorable, but other than that, it's a fine exploration of upward mobility and aspirations, written by an author whose prose never fails to delight.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17554081159115382263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1690592040" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1690592040" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1690592040?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perry" href="/user/show/6354112-perry">Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1690592040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15970138787967013788">"<b>
  <i>On her side</i>
</b>"<br/><b>Come Again? Pardon? Huh?</b><br/><br/>March 8, 2017<br/><br/><i>Open Letter to Baron Fellowes of West Stafford (Lord Julian Fellowes)</i>:<br/><br/> After reading the novel <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26950.The_Custom_of_the_Country' title='The Custom of the Country by Edith Wharton' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Custom of the Country</a>
</b>, I read that you attribute to this novel your success with, among other endeavors, the popular series <i>Downton Abbey</i>, and the part of your speech accepting the 2012 Edith Wharton Lifetime Achievement Award when you said: <blockquote>"It is quite true that I felt this was my book; that the novel was talking to me in a most extreme an</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText15970138787967013788" style="display:none">"<b>
  <i>On her side</i>
</b>"<br /><b>Come Again? Pardon? Huh?</b><br /><br />March 8, 2017<br /><br /><i>Open Letter to Baron Fellowes of West Stafford (Lord Julian Fellowes)</i>:<br /><br /> After reading the novel <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26950.The_Custom_of_the_Country" title="The Custom of the Country by Edith Wharton" rel="nofollow noopener">The Custom of the Country</a>
</b>, I read that you attribute to this novel your success with, among other endeavors, the popular series <i>Downton Abbey</i>, and the part of your speech accepting the 2012 Edith Wharton Lifetime Achievement Award when you said: <blockquote>"It is quite true that I felt this was my book; that the novel was talking to me in a most extreme and immediate way. I think it's a remarkable piece of writing. In Undine Spragg, Wharton has created an anti-heroine absolutely in the same rank as Becky Sharp, Scarlett O'Hara, or Lizzie Eustace. Undine has no values except ambition, greed and desire, and yet through the miracle of Wharton's writing, <u><i>you are on her side</i></u>. That's what's so extraordinary about the book...I decided, largely because of her work, that it was time I wrote something."</blockquote> I agree, in a general way, with everything you state about this novel up until the part about being "on [Undine Spragg's] side." I find the thought that anyone could pull for Undine Spragg quite perplexing and almost troubling. Perhaps I might attribute our difference in opinion to class distinctions: me an attorney who grew up middle class in the American Deep South and you a loaded, landed Baron/author/screenwriter reared in South Kensington and Chiddingly, East Sussex. I do not think though, that this can be pegged simply to the fact that you are English and I am American. At least I hope that is not the case because I do sincerely believe that I am on the side of the angels here. <br /><br />I enjoyed this fine novel as a satire of the upper class society in New York City at the start of the 20th Century. I take heart from the fact that no one in the novel pulls for or is on Undine's side except Undine. Her father cannot even stand her. I cannot think of or imagine a female anti-heroine who is or could be more despicable, callow, vacuous, callous, nauseating, cold, loveless, loathsome, self-centered and inhumane as Undine Spragg. She was spoiled by her parents, threw fits when her father hesitated to bankrupt himself to buy her the next new thing to fit into NYC's upper crust, she married in hopes of money, then, when it was never enough, she abandoned husband and child and prostituted herself for jewelry, long stays in Paris, et cetera, then failed to come home from her vixen adventure to attend to her son or husband when the husband had pneumonia, did not bat an eye upon her husband's awful death, and committed many other moral misdeeds. <br /><br />In short, I could not find one redeeming quality in this b*tch. I wanted her to fall and miserably so in some tangible or measurable way; certainly, she failed in about every part of the moral human character and condition.<br /> <br />By posting this online, I am inviting anyone (of any class, gender, age, and from anywhere) who has read this novel to enlighten me on what I am missing that put Baron Fellowes of West Stafford "on [Undine Spragg's] side." My Lord, hath our moral decadence come to this?<br /><br /><br />Most Respectfully Yours,<br /><br />/s/ <br />Attorney Addled, At A Loss in Alabama<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15970138787967013788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1358926400" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1358926400" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1358926400?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">any reader of serious fiction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1358926400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10332701803813321091">My new favorite writer is Edith Wharton. I have read four of her wonderful novels this year and I intend to read all of the others in time. She is one of the sharpest observers of mankind that I have ever come across. You could believe that she sat and studied the people around her and then drew them in flesh and blood (that often ran red) on the sheets of paper in front of her. They are real, they breathe, and they make me wish to cry with them, comfort them or slap them with a fervor that is g</span>
  <span id="freeText10332701803813321091" style="display:none">My new favorite writer is Edith Wharton. I have read four of her wonderful novels this year and I intend to read all of the others in time. She is one of the sharpest observers of mankind that I have ever come across. You could believe that she sat and studied the people around her and then drew them in flesh and blood (that often ran red) on the sheets of paper in front of her. They are real, they breathe, and they make me wish to cry with them, comfort them or slap them with a fervor that is generally reserved for non-fictional human beings.<br /><br />Undine Spragg is one of the most complex characters ever drawn. Much of what she is can be easily understood by viewing her background and seeing her struggle to haul herself up from the acceptable but somewhat meager beginnings in Apex, NC to the elevated status of a New York society maven. She seems to have no scruples about how she elevates herself, however, and she can recognize none of the greater qualities in people. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[When she has driven her loving and devoted husband to the point of suicide, her reaction is one of wondering why she is always required to pay such a heavy price to obtain those things that are rightfully coming to her (in this case his money). She has so little regard for her child that she is willing to use him as a pawn in a game of ransom, and she will marry or exploit anyone she thinks will push her up the ladder of society. What is sadder still is that she has no recognition of what true genteel society is. She marries into that with Ralph and despises it. Her true match is the conniving, exploitive, and unrefined Moffat, who is also just a social climber. They have one thing in common, all their values are defined by money and position. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Watching Undine claw her way to the top is like watching an automobile accident in the making. You can scream at her to apply the brakes, but she has got too much momentum to ever stop the vehicle. To carry the analogy a step further, if she survives the accident, she will only think she is safer in a bigger, heavier car. She will not really learn to drive any better. <br /><br />Her selfishness is unbelievable (and yet, sorry to say, I have seen women just like her in my own lifetime...want, want, want and never any satisfaction. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ In fact, even when she has Moffatt in the end and all the money she could ever wish, her poor French husband’s family heirloom tapestries hanging on her wall, and the power to manipulate most of society into admitting her because of her money, she is dissatisfied.<br /><br />Wharton’s last poke at Undine is “Even now, however, she was not always happy. She had everything she wanted, but she still felt, at times, that there were other things she might want if she knew about them. And there had been moments lately when she had had to confess to herself that Moffatt did not fit into the picture. At first she had been dazzled by his success and subdued by his authority. He had given her all she had ever wished for,and more than she had ever dreamed of having: he had made up to her for all her failures and blunders, and there were hours when she still felt his dominion and exulted in it. But there were others when she saw his defects and was irritated by them: when his loudness and redness, his misplaced joviality, his familiarity with the servants, his alternating swagger and ceremony with her friends, jarred on perceptions that had developed in her unawares. Now and then she caught herself thinking that his two predecessors--who were gradually becoming merged in her memory--would have said this or that differently, behaved otherwise in such and such a case. And the comparison was almost always to Moffatt's disadvantage.”<br /><br />She will never be happy or satisfied and she will always make others suffer for her desires and shortcomings. Moffatt will be the next to be made miserable in trying to satisfy Undine, but at least Moffatt will be someone who will be armed against her, as he is her kind and her equal. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />While it is tempting to concentrate on Undine, since she is the character that moves all the action forward, one should not forget how beautifully Wharton draws all the other characters in this tale. Ralph Marvell is a stupendous character. He is an iconic picture of the old guard that is being squashed in favor of the nouveau riche. (I thought of Ashley Wilkes in GWTW, a man who has outlived the lifestyle he was intended for) All the qualities that make him exceptional are also the things that now hold him down in the society he inhabits. He has his name and little else and we can clearly see the Dogenets are a family falling from power. Peter Van Degen is a great example of someone who is from the old-school families but is learning to navigate perfectly in the new, less moral world of Wall Street investors, and Undine’s father is someone who seeks to become part of a world that does not improve his lot or make him happier. He was a big fish in a small pond in Apex, in New York he barely knows how to fit into the society he seeks. It is easy to see that if not of Undine, he would have been happily prospering in Apex. Every lesser character, from little Paul Marvell to Ralph’s cousin and sister, are drawn with precision and depth and seem real and essential to the understanding of this world.<br /><br />I could not be more pleased to have added this wonderful book to my growing list of Wharton missives. She is a masterful, insightful, splendid writer; a professional in the art of character study; and an expert in the art of flowing, expressive prose. If you have not come to her yet, you are missing one of the great writers of the early twentieth century.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10332701803813321091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3056910400" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3056910400" />
    <a title="Anna Luce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9429655-anna-luce"><img alt="Anna Luce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604227738p2/9429655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3056910400?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna Luce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna Luce" href="/user/show/9429655-anna-luce">Anna Luce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22605071-the-custom-of-the-country">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=2019-reviews">2019-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=4-favourites">4-favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3056910400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4296753697200780063">★★★★✰ 4 stars <br/><br/><b>Step aside, Becky Sharp. Move over, Scarlett O'Hara...make way for Undine Spragg, the most unscrupulous anti-heroine I have ever encountered</b>. <br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>“[S]he could not conceive that any one could tire of her of whom she had not first tired.”</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>Wharton once again focuses her narrative on <b>a young woman’s unrelenting attempts at social climbing</b>. While Wharton does inject her depiction of Undine Spragg's ‘trials’ with<b> a dose of satire</b> she nevertheless is able to carry out <b>an incisive commentar</b></span>
  <span id="freeText4296753697200780063" style="display:none">★★★★✰ 4 stars <br /><br /><b>Step aside, Becky Sharp. Move over, Scarlett O'Hara...make way for Undine Spragg, the most unscrupulous anti-heroine I have ever encountered</b>. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“[S]he could not conceive that any one could tire of her of whom she had not first tired.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Wharton once again focuses her narrative on <b>a young woman’s unrelenting attempts at social climbing</b>. While Wharton does inject her depiction of Undine Spragg's ‘trials’ with<b> a dose of satire</b> she nevertheless is able to carry out <b>an incisive commentary</b> regarding New York’s ‘high society’. Through her <b>piercing insights into privilege </b>Wharton is able to render a detailed and engaging examination of the intricate customs that prevailed among America’s ‘elite’ society, exemplifying the discordance between their values and their behaviour. Wharton emphasises their sense of entitlement and their idleness. While they often believe themselves to possess the most impeccable manners, <b>readers know just how cut-throat they truly are</b>. <br />Armed with gossip or ready to form conniving schemes, most of them will hesitate at nothing<b> in order to augment their wealth and reputation</b> (ideally ruining someone's life in the process). <b>Marriages are business manoeuvres</b> and one makes friends on the basis of whether they might be later on be put to good use (‘networking’ is everything for these people). <br />By bringing together these different themes and subjects—<b>marriage, divorce, class, wealth</b>—Wharton is able to present her readers with <b>a nuanced and in-depth examination of New York's upper crust</b>. <br /><br />As a character in the novel observes, Undine Spragg is the <i>“monstrously perfect result of the system: the completest proof of its triumph”</i>. Undine, who was raised by two loving parents who spoiled her from a young age, possesses <b>a solipsistic worldview</b> and her <b>values are exceedingly materialistic</b>. <br />Undine is an appalling protagonist. She is <b>Lily Bart's monstrous little sister</b>. We first ‘meet’ Undine when <b>she still seems to be a simple, if pampered, ‘country’ girl</b>. Soon however we begin to see that in spite of her simplicity (she definitely lacks Miss Bart's charisma and acumen) Undine Spragg is<b> entirely egocentric and lacks both self-awareness and empathy</b>. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“It never occurred to her that other people's lives went on when they were out of her range of vision.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />As noted by the narrative and the various characters, <b>Undine's conceitedness, as well as her perpetual sense of boredom, may be the likely result of her upbringing</b>. Her parents' leniency definitely played its role in making Undine feel as if she should only be concerned with her own happiness, and to be truly happy she has to marry well. <br />Undine believes that as long as can enjoy an extravagant lifestyle and be favoured within certain circles, she won't be bored. As much as <b>I loathed Undine</b>—for her selfishness, her lack of creativity, and for her frivolous tastes—I was always aware that she did grow up in <b>a society that values appearances</b>. <br />Undine was never made to feel as if she needed to cultivate any real interest. <b>Her main concern are her own beauty and reputation</b>, the two means through which she will be able to find a satisfactory match.<br />It shouldn't be surprising then that Undine becomes <b>a woman who is thoroughly disinterested in the lives of others</b>. She sees no reason why she should be preoccupied with her husband's ‘menial’ work. She is unable to see why she should be held accountable for other people's misery. <br />There was something <b>oddly compelling</b> about Undine's determination not to allow her desires to be comprised by anyone or anything. She is more than willing to have affairs, lie, drive her husband(s) and family into debt, and blackmail and manipulate others. <br />While the narrative definitely accentuates<b> Undine’s cherubic appearance </b>(from her creamy complexion to her beautiful golden locks) readers are made aware of what lies beneath her rosy surface: <b>Undine's vision of happiness is rather limited</b>. She lacks imagination, so much so that she often merely tries to emulate the women around her.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Her entrances were always triumphs; but they had no sequel.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />And while I certainly thought her to be a horrible person (her behaviour is reprehensible) there was a part of me that found<b> her egocentrism and cruelty</b> to be strangely compelling. Whether she is merely a product of environment or innately selfish, her total <b>self-absorption was transfixing</b>.<br />Wharton portrays a scathing picture of her society: were <i>“the average American looks down on his wife”</i>, were women's sense of self is dictated by a cult of aspiration, were<b> marriages are entirely transactional</b>, and were <b>young individuals are trapped by old traditions and customs</b>.<br />In spite of Undine's many romances, there is little if any love to be found within the pages of <i>The Custom of the Country</i>. And maybe that's for the best given that <b>Undine is no heroine</b>. <br />While I certainly didn't find this novel to be as moving as Wharton's <br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3008131763" rel="nofollow noopener">The Age of Innocence</a>, and Undine's misadventures lack the poignancy of Lily's ones in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3017590033" rel="nofollow noopener">The House of Mirth</a>, I would still recommend this. <b>Wharton's percipient prose</b>, her<b> sophisticated use of satire</b>, vividly renders the customs and values of New York's high class.<br /><br /><a href="https://wishfullyreading.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Read more reviews on my blog</a> / / / <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/9429655-anna-luce" rel="nofollow noopener">View all my reviews on Goodreads</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4296753697200780063" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_950699936" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

What a fatal, restless passion you have--not for men themselves--but for their admiration, and for the money and possessions they might bring you.   <span id="freeText16896502232976171528" style="display:none">Alas, Undine! What a fatal, restless passion you have--not for men themselves--but for their admiration, and for the money and possessions they might bring you. You do so love your ropes of pearls!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440341912i/15963978._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And how utterly miserable you make yourself and everyone around you. Can anyone in this glittering world ever satisfy your insatiable lust for more and still more things? Will you settle for a fine apartment, perhaps on Fifth Avenue--surely the West Side is not enough? <br> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440341912i/15963979._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Or perhaps you'd fancy a grand Hôtel in France and a Château in the countryside? But no, the tapestries are so dusty and it's all such a <i>bore</i>!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440341912i/15963980._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And who is the man who can satisfy you? The sensitive Ralph Marvell from a patrician New York family?<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440341912i/15963981._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Or the handsome, determined French aristocrat, Raymond De Chelles?<br> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440341912i/15963982._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>But then...there is the crude new American, Elmer Moffatt, a financier and social climber every bit as ruthless as Undine herself... <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440341912i/15963983._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The Custom of the Country is an irresistible mix of grand soap opera and social satire simply packed with unforgettable characters, locations, and glorious descriptions. Home furnishings, architecture, dress silks or feathered hats--every detail is caressed with an acquisitive eye. <br><br>As Undine climbs ever higher I delighted in Glided Age Washington Square with it's old-money families...<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440353015i/15965353._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Elegant, intimate salons filled with fine paintings, china vases and delicate gilded chairs...<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440353015i/15965354._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The fading grandeur of Old Europe is keenly drawn, as is the invasion of New Money Americans eager to appropriate the 'finer things' without really understanding them.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440353015i/15965355._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I flew through The Custom of the Country in two sittings and I can't wait to read more Edith Wharton. <br><br>P.S. The portraits are all by John Singer Sargent, reputedly the inspiration for Claud Walsingham Popple, who paints a portrait of Undine. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7861377136314121588">Edith Wharton understood a certain type of woman as well or better than anyone who ever wrote a book. Undine was narcissistic, beautiful, manipulative, clever (but not overly intelligent or curious), and, above all, ambitious. She was more ruthless and eviscerating than a mafia don.<br/><br/>Eventually, one of her captivated followers might notice her complete lack of concern for anyone but herself and her lack of interest in anything other than shopping or dining. Some even began to find her boring, but </span>
  <span id="freeText7861377136314121588" style="display:none">Edith Wharton understood a certain type of woman as well or better than anyone who ever wrote a book. Undine was narcissistic, beautiful, manipulative, clever (but not overly intelligent or curious), and, above all, ambitious. She was more ruthless and eviscerating than a mafia don.<br /><br />Eventually, one of her captivated followers might notice her complete lack of concern for anyone but herself and her lack of interest in anything other than shopping or dining. Some even began to find her boring, but as a reader I was never bored by her. She was a fascinating piece of work and the book is absolutely wonderful.</span>
